Predicted Answer: A: The description in this question is most consistent with sepsis due to Neisseria meningitidis infection. Phenol testing of blood at 90°C will result in a positive test if the blood contains certain endotoxins. When the blood from a patient with N. meningitidis sepsis is exposed to phenol, it can undergo thermal instability, which then prompts the release of lipopolysaccharide (LPS) subunits from the bacterial cell wall into the bloodstream. The presence of LPS is what leads to the endotoxin-driven reaction that ultimately manifests in the clinical symptoms and laboratory findings listed here.

Predicted Answer: A: If a patient moves from a supine to a standing position, he will experience a shift of blood away from the lower extremities toward the head and upper extremities due to gravity, resulting in an increase in right atrial pressure as blood accumulates in the thoracic cavity. This maneuver does not directly affect pulmonary capillary wedge (PCW) pressure since PCW is primarily determined by left ventricular end-diastolic volume. The slope of the pacemaker potential would be reduced only if the patient experienced a decrease in intracellular Ca2+ levels within cardiac cells, as occurs with hypocalcemia or certain anesthetic agents. Finally, diastolic filling time would not change in this scenario since it is primarily determined by heart rate and contractility rather than by posture. (Choice B) PCW pressure is the same as left atrial pressure when there are no gradients across the mitral valve. The slope of the pacemaker potential refers to the slope of phase 4 depolarization, which would be increased in hypocalcemia.

This question asks which risk factor is responsible for this patient's non-Hodgkin’s lymphoma. The correct answer is A: Travel to Africa. There are four choices and only one of them is associated with an increased incidence of non-Hodgkin’s lymphoma, specifically the diffuse large B-cell variant. Infection with Epstein-Barr virus (EBV), also known as human herpesvirus 4, leads to infectious mononucleosis, but chronic infection has been associated with an increased risk for development of NHL, including the diffuse large B cell variant. Individuals infected with EBV are more likely to have visited Africa and thus be exposed to malaria, which facilitates transmission of EBV.

Predicted Answer: A) The child has signs of status asthmaticus and severe asthma exacerbation requiring mechanical ventilation for respiratory failure, which is a life-threatening condition. Intravenous magnesium sulfate was given as an adjunctive treatment to albuterol nebulization in the management of this patient. However, there are specific situations where patients with asthma can present in shock after receiving albuterol treatment. The most important clue regarding this child's presentation lies in the clinical improvement that occurred immediately after he received magnesium sulfate. Magnesium is a bronchodilator and also has an inotropic effect on the heart. Therefore, a patient who presents in shock (low blood pressure) with severe asthma exacerbation may benefit from intravenous magnesium because it can help to improve cardiac contractility and vascular tone. In this case, after administration of magnesium sulfate, the child's vital signs improved significantly, including blood pressure (normalized), pulse rate decreased, and respirations decreased as well. Therefore, there is no need to mechanically ventilate him (option A). The patient does not have a pneumothorax because no crackles or rubs are heard on physical examination of chest in addition to the absence of any abnormalities in lung fields following administration of albuterol nebulization with ipratropium bromide. Hence, performing needle thoracostomy would be inappropriate (option B). The clinical improvement that occurred after magnesium sulfate was given indicates that it is working well and there is no need for bronchoscopy (option C) or additional dose of methylprednisolone (option D).

Explanation: This patient has chronic alcoholism complicated by homelessness. His lab values are consistent with alcoholic ketoacidosis (AKA) which is defined as having an elevated anion gap and elevated ketones. Ethanol can be used as a antidote for methanol or ethylene glycol poisoning in AKA, although this is the less effective of the two treatments (fomepizole being the better treatment). Choice C is incorrect because naloxone only works for opiate overdose. It will worsen ethanol intoxication as it is a competitive antagonist at ethanol's receptor site (the GABA A receptor). Fomepizole, choice D, is better than ethanol for treating methanol or ethylene glycol poisoning but it would not have any additional benefit in the treatment of alcoholic ketoacidosis.

Predicted Answer: A: The NNT = Number of subjects needed to be treated in order to prevent one bad outcome. For the primary endpoint of death from cardiac causes, there were 134 deaths on treatment with medication 1 and 210 deaths on treatment with medication 2. Therefore, the total number of deaths is 344 (or 3,000 x [134 + 210]/3,000). The NNT = 344/[(210 - 134)/210] = 5.

Predicted Answer: A) This patient is a primigravid female at 35 weeks' gestation with no prior serious medical illness who presents in labor without fetal heartbeats detectable by the fetal doppler monitor. Ultrasound shows decreased amniotic fluid levels and no evidence of fetal movement, respiration, or heart activity. The infant weighs 2296 g (5 lb 1 oz) at birth and has no visible malformations. Given that there was a normal ultrasound performed at 22 weeks' gestation, this scenario may represent an in utero demise of the fetus likely due to a placental abruption. A karyotype on the amniotic fluid is not needed because the fetus's death occurred after the amniocentesis (obtained at 15-18 weeks), and it is highly unlikely that the fetus would have died without any other signs or symptoms of distress in utero. Photographs, x-ray imaging, and MRI may be useful in determining the cause of death but are not a priority given the clinical presentation. Obtaining consent for parental genetic testing is also not needed in this case because there was no indication that the fetus had an underlying genetic disorder, which would have been identified by the prior normal ultrasound at 22 weeks' gestation. Thus, the most appropriate step in management of this scenario is a postmortem examination to determine the cause of fetal demise and placental abruption.

Retrieved Content: 131.1.3 Effects of Medications
Predicted Answer: A: Risperidone can cause agranulocytosis. This is an uncommon but dangerous side effect of this medication and other atypical antipsychotics. Agranulocytosis is a potentially life-threatening decrease in granulocytes, especially neutrophils. Typically, it manifests as fever, fatigue, sore throat, or the presence of an infection (such as pneumonia). The mechanism behind this reaction isn't well understood, but it may be related to the drug's ability to inhibit dopamine D2 receptors and increase the levels of dopamine in the brain. Other side effects associated with risperidone include orthostatic hypotension, sedation, dizziness, somnolence, nausea, constipation, tremor, anxiety, and QT prolongation. (This makes B incorrect.) Risperidone may also cause gynecomastia in men because it increases prolactin levels. However, the patient in this Q is male, making C incorrect. The weight loss is a side effect of the SSRI fluoxetine. Therefore, D is also incorrect.

Predicted Answer: A: The description of this girl's illness is most suggestive of typhoid fever, which results from infection with Salmonella Typhi. Explanation: This patient has a presentation consistent with oral candidiasis. Oral candidiasis is caused by Candida albicans and often presents as white pseudomembranes that can be wiped away, leaving an erythematous base. The most appropriate medication for this patient would be nystatin, which disrupts cell membrane permeability. Nystatin works by binding to sterols in the fungal cell membrane, thus disrupting its function, leading to the death of the organism. Choices B and C are incorrect because they refer to the antifungal medications amphotericin B (which inhibits microtubule formation) and ketoconazole (which inhibits 14-alpha-demethylase), respectively. Choice D is also incorrect as it refers to the mechanism of action of echinocandins, such as caspofungin, which inhibit beta-glucan synthase.

Note: Candida albicans is a fungus that can cause yeast infections in various parts of the body including the mouth and vagina. It has also been implicated in systemic candidiasis, an invasive fungal infection that can occur in immunocompromised hosts such as patients with HIV/AIDS or who are on chemotherapy and have neutropenia.
The best answer is A.

Predicted Answer: A: The patient's symptoms are suggestive of schistosomiasis, which is caused by Schistosoma species of flatworms. These worms have a complex life cycle involving freshwater snails as an intermediate host and humans (and other mammals) as the final host. Infection with these organisms leads to chronic inflammation in organs such as the liver, intestines, and urinary bladder. Eggs laid by the female worm can cause tissue damage in the affected organ. The presence of eggs with lateral spines in the stool culture is consistent with schistosomiasis. This organism can also be associated with a leukocytosis (increased number of white blood cells) that contains eosinophils, granulomatous inflammation, and periportal fibrosis in organs such as the liver. The exposure most likely to have played a role in the development of this patient's symptoms was her exposure to freshwater snails while in Uganda.

Predicted Answer: A: The patient has developed a case of recurrent gonorrhea. Gonorrhea is caused by Neisseria gonorrhoeae and is known for lacking lasting immunity; 75% of patients do not have protective antibodies against the next infection even if they were previously infected. This lack of lasting immunity is due to the ability of N. gonorrhoeae to change its surface pili protein. This variation in antigen makes it difficult for the body to develop a lasting immune response, and thus the patient will likely be susceptible again after the initial course of treatment has resolved. (A) is correct because pili proteins are involved with adhesion of organisms to surfaces. They can vary due to their antigenic nature. The exotoxins released from bacteria generally refer to toxins that act outside the host cell; these toxins can cause systemic effects such as diarrhea, fever, and vomiting. This does not apply directly to this question and (A) is the best choice, eliminating (B), (C), and (D).

Predicted Answer: A: Magnesium toxicity can cause absent deep tendon reflexes, as well as other neurologic symptoms such as confusion and slurred speech. It is treated by administering calcium gluconate because it will help block the effects of magnesium at neuromuscular junctions. Labetalol may be given to reduce blood pressure but does not have any significant side effect profile that would lead us away from using it in this case, so choice B is incorrect. Lorazepam can also be used to calm patients who are agitated due to their pain or discomfort; however, the patient's deep tendon reflexes will return if the magnesium toxicity is treated with calcium gluconate, and there may be some residual sedation effect from lorazepam that we might not want in this case. Therefore, choice C is incorrect. Nerve conduction studies are diagnostic tests that would help identify nerve damage but do not play a role in treating absent deep tendon reflexes due to magnesium toxicity.

Predicted Answer: A: This patient has Klinefelter syndrome, characterized by hypergonadotropic hypogonadism and azoospermia. The genetic abnormality associated with this condition is XXY, as the testes have extra X chromosomes. If the error occurred in spermatogenesis at any point after the sex chromatin determination (i.e., meiosis), it would be expected to be a Y chromosome loss, resulting in 45,X cells. This is seen in patients with Klinefelter syndrome as well as other non-disjunction errors such as Turner syndrome (45,X). The presence of XY cells would indicate that the error occurred at the spermatogonium stage; thus, A is incorrect. Since the patient's testes have only XX cells, the error must occur after the sex chromatin determination in meiosis, so B, C, and D are also incorrect.
Correct Answer: A

Retrieved Content: the septic hip joint is not capable of complete evacuation ofharmful inflammatory elements and is painful. Repeated as-piration of the knee or ankle may be effective, but surgicaldrainage of the involved hip joint is the standard of care.
147.3 Diagnosis and Syndromes
Predicted Answer: A: The most likely diagnosis for this patient is slipped capital femoral epiphysis (SCFE), which is a disorder that occurs during adolescent growth spurts where the proximal portion of the femur fails to undergo normal apophyseal development and begins to slip posteriorly, causing pain in the affected limb. SCFE has a predilection for obese prepubescent males. The best initial management for this condition is casting or crutch ambulation to immobilize the hip and prevent further slippage. A Pavlik harness may be used in infants with developmental dysplasia of the hip; however, it would not be appropriate in this patient due to his age. Supportive therapy alone without immobilization could lead to further slippage of the femoral head. Surgical pinning is typically considered as a secondary procedure when conservative management has failed and further slippage of the femur is noted on imaging studies.

Predicted Answer: A: This patient's presentation is most consistent with carcinoid syndrome, a condition caused by an excess of serotonin, often resulting from metastatic carcinoids tumors in the liver of patients with neuroendocrine cancer. The mucoid strings are due to the presence of secretin and other hormonal products. These secretions inhibit peristalsis (resulting in bloating and crampy pain) and stimulate pancreatic secretion (resulting in diarrhea). The excess serotonin also increases gut blood flow, resulting in the patient's weight loss. His rash may have been a cutaneous manifestation of the syndrome. This condition is associated with elevated levels of vasoactive intestinal polypeptide (VIP), which causes vasodilation, increased gut blood flow, and diarrhea.

Predicted Answer: A)  Torsades de pointes are a specific type of polymorphic VT that can occur in patients who have prolonged QT intervals on an ECG. This patient's symptoms of nausea, vomiting, and abdominal discomfort may indicate hypomagnesemia or hypokalemia (both of which would be expected after surgery, given the patient is taking only a multivitamin supplement). Hypokalemia and/or hypomagnesemia can cause prolongation of the QT interval. This patient's symptoms of numbness around his mouth and spasms in his hand suggest hypocalcemia as well. Hypocalcemia, along with hypokalemia or hypomagnesemia, is expected to result in a prolonged QT interval, which could lead to torsades de pointes. Predicted Answer: (D) The endpoint in this question is survival time. The earlier detection of GBM, as a result of screening MRI, led to an increase in survival time because it was based on when the diagnosis was made rather than the actual outcome (death from GBM). This phenomenon is known as lead-time bias. Lead-time bias occurs when there appears to be an improvement in outcomes due to an earlier detection of disease, whereas there really has not been any change in patient survival or quality of life. It would have been incorrect to answer D if the endpoint had been mortality rate rather than survival time.

This patient has lead poisoning due to pica eating of peeling paint. The physical exam findings are classic for lead poisoning. The peripheral smear shows basophilic stippling from aggregation of ribosomes (the "stippling") and the small, pale red blood cells are consistent with microcytosis. Choices B and D are steps in heme synthesis that occur later than choice C and involve different enzymes. Predicted Answer: A: The American Academy of Pediatrics recommends that infants born to mothers who received antiretroviral therapy during pregnancy receive a single dose of zidovudine given within 12 hours of birth as part of the general prophylaxis for HIV exposure. Predicted Answer: A: The child has a disorder of amino acid metabolism. The most common disorder associated with hyperphenylalaninemia is PKU (see question 1), which can be treated by dietary restriction of phenylalanine. This patient's inability to decrease his phenylalanine concentration and the presence of generalized hypotonia, blue eyes, pale skin, blonde hair, and markedly elevated serum prolactin levels should prompt consideration of a related but distinct disorder: DYT (Dopa-responsive dystonia). In this condition, deficiency of dihydropteridine reductase leads to the inability of phenylalanine-derived neurotransmitters to be properly catabolized. These patients have normal PKU screening tests because they can adequately convert phenylalanine to tyrosine but cannot then inactivate dopamine, which causes dystonia and hypotonia, as well as hyperprolactinemia. Deficiency of this enzyme is usually due to mutations involving tetrahydrobiopterin (BH4) supplementation has been used with great success in these patients because BH4 acts as a cofactor for dihydropteridine reductase.

Retrieved Content: The diagnosis of meningitis depends on lumbar puncture
for examination of the cerebrospinal fluid (cell count, chem-istry and cultures). Imaging techniques (ultrasonography,magnetic resonance imaging) are mainly useful for prognosisor to establish whether there are complications.
Predicted Answer: A: This patient has symptoms and signs of multiple sclerosis (MS), which is an inflammatory demyelinating disease of the central nervous system (CNS). The findings listed in this question include internuclear ophthalmoplegia, hyperreflexia, clonus, and bladder dysfunction, among others. These are all common presenting complaints associated with MS. The MRI study also most likely would show lesions characteristic of multiple sclerosis. Therefore, if a lumbar puncture is performed on this patient, the cerebrospinal fluid analysis will be consistent with an inflammatory demyelinating disease process (MS). In patients suspected of having multiple sclerosis, there are several tests that can confirm or exclude the diagnosis. Cerebrospinal fluid electrophoresis can demonstrate oligoclonal IgG bands. Normally, patients with multiple sclerosis do not have oligoclonal bands in their CSF because it is not able to pass through the intact blood-brain barrier (BBB). Oligoclonal bands are found only within the CNS and are present in 90% of patients with MS. Therefore, if no bands are found on CSF electrophoresis, this can help exclude a diagnosis of multiple sclerosis. Thus, choice C is correct. Choices A, B, and D are incorrect because they are associated with viral infections, bacterial meningitis, and subacute combined degeneration, respectively, all of which would not be consistent with the clinical findings described in this question. Predicted Answer: A: The p-value of 0.052 indicates a statistically insignificant result. Choice A misrepresents the meaning of a p-value. If there were 100 studies and this one was similar enough that it came out the same way as the others 5.2% of the time, then its p-value would have to be equal to 0.052. However, you must consider all 100 experiments and what p-values they had. The study in question would need a p-value greater than 0.052 (because it is less likely than the other results) for this choice to be correct. Choice B misrepresents what it means for a result to be statistically significant (answer D). If you were going to test the hypothesis that statin A is more effective, then B would be true. However, because we are testing that statin A is less effective, you must reverse the statement and its truth value in answer B. That results in an incorrect choice since you are looking for a 4.8% chance of the opposite happening (of it being due to chance) rather than a 5.2% chance of the hypothesis being true. Choice C misrepresents the meaning of the p-value. If there were a 94.8% chance that statin A was more effective, then there would be a 5.2% chance that B is more effective, and since this is not what we are looking for, you can eliminate answer C.

The best answer is A.

82.4.5 Infection
Predicted Answer: A: The combination of an elevated calcium level and eosinophilic casts are consistent with multiple myeloma. However, this patient also has a renal biopsy showing large eosinophilic casts. This would be more consistent with a diagnosis of light chain disease (or lambda or kappa chain disease) which is seen in the context of multiple myeloma. Light chain disease can cause a variety of manifestations including damage to the kidneys (known as "myeloma kidney") where the abnormal monoclonal protein is excreted and accumulates in the kidneys and causes damage. Hemolysis due to mechanical destruction of red cells by fibrin strands (also known as schistocytes) is seen in conditions that lead to microangiopathic hemolytic anemia, which can be seen in myeloma kidney among other conditions. The presence of schistocytes on a peripheral smear would indicate active microangiopathic hemolysis and thus is consistent with the patient's findings.

Note: Choices A through D were all somewhat plausible answers for different reasons but the best answer was C.

Predicted Answer: A: While all of these screening tests may be valuable in certain patients, they are not required for pregnancy screening unless indicated by patient risk factors. Hepatitis A is not a bloodborne infection and therefore does not need to be screened for unless specifically indicated in the patient's history. Hepatitis C, N. gonorrhea, and C. trachomatis are all STIs that should only be tested for in patients at high risk (e.g., those with multiple sexual partners or who have a history of STDs). HIV screening is recommended once during pregnancy as part of routine prenatal care to identify patients who may benefit from antiretroviral therapy. Syphilis testing is also recommended, although it can likely be done using nontreponemal tests such as the VDRL and RPR in addition to treponemal tests such as the FTA-ABS and TPPA (the specific test choice will depend on the patient's serologic response). Hepatitis B is a bloodborne pathogen that does not require screening unless the patient has a history of blood transfusions or needle sharing, though vaccination against this virus should be offered to all patients. Therefore, while screening for HIV and syphilis are recommended for all pregnant women, other STIs (hepatitis A is actually an enteric pathogen) do not need to be screened unless the patient's specific risk factors warrant such testing. The correct answer is therefore (A).
Correct Answer: A

Predicted Answer: (B)  The symptoms described in this case are typical of paranoid personality disorder (PPD), which is part of Cluster A in DSM-IV-TR. One must always look for characteristic patterns of thought and behavior when a patient presents with a complaint that cannot be explained by a primary diagnosis. Individuals with PPD are suspicious, distrust others, and tend to overestimate their abilities. They are often perceived as being rigid and critical. If the symptoms described in this case could belong to another personality disorder, it would be paranoid personality disorder’s "cluster cousin," the passive-aggressive or negativistic personality disorder. The symptoms of obsessive-compulsive personality disorder would not match those described. Individuals with that disorder tend to be overly anxious about orderliness and perfectionism. Antisocial personality disorder (also called psychopathic personality) is a cluster B disorder characterized by aggressive, manipulative, and unemotional behavior toward others. Schizotypal personality disorder, choice A, is another Cluster A disorder characterized by odd behavior, eccentricities, or social withdrawal. However, it does not include suspicion of being persecuted as in the case described. (D)  would be an incorrect answer if no other diagnosis fits a cluster pattern. (Knowledge level II)

Predicted Answer: A: The diagram provided is a Darrow-Yannet diagram that plots total body fluid volume and osmolality. In this patient with diarrhea and vomiting, it appears that there is significant dehydration leading to decreased blood volume (green dotted line indicating hypovolemia). This suggests that this patient will likely have an increase in secretion of substances aimed at increasing water reabsorption, such as B-type natriuretic peptide (BNP) which promotes sodium excretion and water retention. The other options are not correct because they either do not promote fluid retention or could actually decrease fluid retention: bradykinin increases vascular permeability and has vasodilatory effects; renin is involved in the regulation of blood pressure but primarily through increasing sodium reabsorption, which may not be as beneficial in this patient's scenario where water loss is prominent; and vasoactive intestinal peptide can also increase secretion of fluids and electrolytes into the intestines. However, its primary effect is vasodilation, and would not be expected to increase fluid retention in a hypovolemic state like the one described.

The best answer is A.

Retrieved Content: blood. Increased glycolytic enzyme activity is re-sponsible for increased consumption of glucose and galactoseand increased levels of adenosine triphosphate (ATP).
Predicted Answer: A
Explanation: This patient most likely has a condition of chronic anemia due to thalassemia major. The peripheral smear findings in this case would be consistent with thalassemia as the echinocytes are indicative of chronic hemolysis and there is no evidence of other causes such as glucose-6-phosphate dehydrogenase (G6PD) deficiency, which also produces schistocytes when oxidized. Thalassemia is caused by mutations that affect globin synthesis, and most notably the beta-globin subunit. The rate-limiting step in heme biosynthesis is catalyzed by the enzyme delta-aminolevulinic acid (ALA) synthase. This enzyme has been shown to be inhibited by negative feedback from ATP as well as other factors; therefore, choice A is correct and choices B and D are incorrect based on this mechanism of action. Note that choice C might seem plausible since protein kinase A activity activates the enzyme in response to low energy states (e.g., AMP). However, this effect occurs further downstream and does not affect the rate-limiting step.
The best answer is A.

Predicted Answer: A: The absence of a palpable pulse during inspiration is a classic sign for cardiac tamponade. Retrieved Content: hypothyroidism, galactosemia, adrenal hyperplasia (cah), bi-otinidase deficiency, sickle cell disease, cystic fibrosis areperformed by immunoassays, radioimmunoassay (RIA), flu-oroimmunoassay (FIA), and enzyme-linked immunosorbentassay (ELISA) techniques. Predicted Answer: A: A person with pernicious anemia has atrophic gastritis and vitamin B12 deficiency. This condition is due to antibodies against parietal cells in the stomach and intrinsic factor, which are necessary for vitamin B12 absorption. The macrocytic anemia and poor balance (neuropathy) result from the lack of vitamin B12. The patient's hypothyroidism medication did not contribute to her symptoms. Islet cell cytoplasmic antibodies (ICA) target islet cells in the pancreas, as seen in type 1 diabetes mellitus; deamidated gliadin peptide is an antigen that causes celiac disease; and smooth muscle targets are involved in a condition known as scleroderma. Therefore, (A) is correct. (B), (C), and (D) are incorrect.

## Step 1: Identify the patient's symptoms and laboratory findings.
The patient presents with tingling in her feet and poor balance, indicating neuropathy. Laboratory studies show macrocytic anemia and normal thyroid hormone levels.

## Step 2: Relate the symptoms to possible underlying conditions.
The combination of neuropathy and macrocytic anemia suggests a condition related to vitamin B12 deficiency.

## Step 3: Determine the cause of the vitamin B12 deficiency.
Given the presence of atrophic gastritis, it is likely that the patient has pernicious anemia, which results from antibodies targeting parietal cells in the stomach and intrinsic factor necessary for vitamin B12 absorption.

## Step 4: Identify the correct target of antibodies based on the condition determined in step 3.
The antibodies are most likely targeting parietal cells in the stomach.

## Step 5: Match the target identified in step 4 to the answer choices provided.
Option C, Parietal cells, is the correct choice.

The final answer is: $\boxed{C}$

Predicted Answer: A: The serum osmolality is elevated and urine osmolality is low. This suggests that there must be an issue with concentrating the urine, which is best explained by a deficiency in ADH production (B). If the patient’s condition was due to a defect in the kidneys’ ability to respond to ADH (C), then serum would be normal or decreased and urine would be elevated. Choice A can be eliminated because it suggests that there is an overproduction of ADH, which would lead to more concentrated urine, not less concentrated. Choice D refers to renal tubular dysfunction; this condition involves metabolic alkalosis, hypokalemia, low magnesium levels, and a specific pattern on the urine Na+ vs. K+ excretion plot called the “spaghetti and meatball” effect (where sodium reabsorption and potassium excretion are both increased). This will not be seen in conjunction with an elevated serum osmolality.

Predicted Answer: A
Explanation: This patient likely has diabetes insipidus due to the high drainage output following his pancreatoduodenectomy. This condition is characterized by an inability of the kidneys to reabsorb water. In this case, because of the lack of exocrine function from his pancreas following surgery, antidiuretic hormone cannot be converted into its active form, resulting in a large amount of dilute urine. The treatment for diabetes insipidus is replacement with free water. This patient's sodium level is elevated at 154 mEq/L, indicating the presence of high volume, low concentration loss of fluid (diluted blood). Because of this, he would require IV replacement with 0.9% saline solution in order to avoid further diluting his serum sodium levels. The other options provided would not correct this patient's hyponatremic and hypovolemic state.

Predicted Answer: Explanation: D This question tests your understanding of how sensitivity affects statistical power and is an example of why the "specificity" trap exists in statistics and medicine. The test's specificity is 0.9 (90%), which means that when this new screening test says a patient does not have the disease, they actually do not have it 90% of the time. This is a very good screen, but it also means that only 45 out of 50 patients who are truly diseased would be detected by this test and would therefore be positive for the test. Therefore, the sensitivity is 0.9 (90%). If you were to decrease the specificity of this screening test to 70%, then more patients with no disease would be falsely said to have it. However, if this new screening test still correctly identified 45 out of every 50 diseased individuals as being positive for the test, then it now has a sensitivity of 0.9 (90%). Therefore, the false positives due to increased numbers of people being falsely diagnosed with disease would increase the total number of true and false positives to 58 out of every 100 patients tested. The statistical power is therefore calculated as follows: $\frac{45}{50} \times \frac{13}{42}$ , which equals 0.81 or 81%. If you choose answer D, make sure that you know why it is correct and understand the difference between sensitivity and specificity.
The best answer is D.
Correct Answer: D

Predicted Answer: A: Warfarin-induced skin necrosis is a serious complication that occurs in patients on warfarin who have underlying deficiency of protein C. This condition tends to occur within the first week after starting anticoagulation, and it can lead to significant morbidity and mortality. The patient described has signs consistent with this diagnosis (painful enlarging bruises, skin ulceration). He also has characteristics that would make him more likely to be deficient in protein C: diabetes mellitus, mitral regurgitation, and atrial fibrillation. Protein C is a vitamin K-dependent clotting factor that serves as an anticoagulant when it is activated. Therefore, the use of warfarin will decrease its activity even further, increasing the risk for this patient to develop skin necrosis. This condition can be life-threatening if not recognized and treated promptly. Intravenous administration of protein C concentrate is effective in treating this condition by replenishing the deficient clotting factor and helping to prevent further coagulopathy. The use of hyperbaric oxygen is indicated in decompression sickness, carbon monoxide poisoning, severe anemia with acute blood loss, and refractory sepsis (a condition not described). Argatroban is a direct thrombin inhibitor that can be used as an alternative anticoagulant when warfarin cannot be used (e.g., when the INR may take several days to normalize or when patients have liver disease), but it does not treat skin necrosis caused by warfarin. Tranexamic acid reduces bleeding in patients with hemophilia and helps prevent blood clot formation after cardiac surgery, but it will not improve this patient's condition. The best answer is B.

Retrieved Content: 55.5.1 Growth Disorders
Predicted Answer: A: This patient presents with features consistent with Sotos syndrome, which is characterized by excessive growth during infancy and early childhood due to an activating mutation in a receptor tyrosine kinase that regulates cell growth. The most characteristic feature of this disorder is accelerated growth rate; affected children grow faster than their peers for the first 2-3 years of life, but experience normal or slightly delayed pubertal development. Affected individuals may also exhibit intellectual disability and facial dysmorphism (frontal bossing). A similar presentation is seen in patients with Weaver syndrome, which shares similar genetic mutations. In both disorders, accelerated growth rate results from the overactivation of mTOR signaling pathway. The mTOR pathway controls cellular processes such as cell growth and protein synthesis. While there are some differences in physical features between Sotos syndrome and Weaver syndrome (with those affected by the latter disorder exhibiting more pronounced features of acromegaly), both disorders result in accelerated growth during childhood that is not accompanied by a similarly increased rate of bone elongation. Consequently, these children often exhibit disproportionately short limbs with normal length axial skeleton at an early age. This patient’s presentation is most consistent with Sotos syndrome; thus, the correct answer is A.

The best answer is A.

Retrieved Content: bradycardia, and arrhythmias that can result in life-threatening hemodynamic instability and cardiac arrest [108].
Predicted Answer: A: Quinidine is a class IA anti-arrhythmic drug that blocks sodium channels and prolongs repolarization. However, it also has anticholinergic activity and can cause tinnitus and headache in high doses. It does not shorten phase 3 repolarization but instead prolongs action potential duration. Class I B drugs like lidocaine are more likely to be used for ventricular arrhythmias as they increase the threshold for arrhythmias and have minimal effect on AV conduction. Quinidine is less effective in suppressing automaticity than sodium channel blockers of class IB, which are more potent at high doses but have a narrower therapeutic index. Class I A drugs like quinidine are safer to use in life-threatening conditions such as torsades de pointes due to their proarrhythmic effect and minimal effect on conduction.

Retrieved Content: phenomenon is caused by the desensitization ofthe cardiovascular system to catecholamines by the critical ill-ness and relative or absolute adrenal insufficiency.
Predicted Answer: A
Explanation: The sudden change in heart rate and blood pressure post-exercise is due to a baroreceptor reflex. Baroreceptors are located within the carotid sinus and in other locations throughout the arterial system (notably the pulmonary arteries). They detect changes in blood pressure, and this information is transmitted to the medulla oblongata, where it causes an appropriate response. The response includes activation of the sympathetic nervous system to increase heart rate and blood pressure if they are too low, or its inactivation along with activation of the parasympathetic nervous system (vagal response) when they are too high. This reflex is responsible for most of the day-to-day regulation of blood pressure and was a significant reason why James Black won the Nobel Prize in 1988 for developing propranolol, which acts as an adrenergic blocker to prevent this effect.
The best answer is A.
Correct Answer: C

Predicted Answer: A: This question requires an understanding of cranial nerves and their functions. The patient described has difficulty swallowing both solids and liquids, which suggests a problem with his esophageal swallow mechanism. In this case, the vagus nerve (CN X) is most likely involved because it provides motor innervation to the muscles of the pharynx, which are responsible for swallowing. Swallowing is a coordinated process that involves several cranial nerves (CNs), but CN X is primarily involved in this patient’s symptoms due to his difficulty initiating esophageal swallows. The other options can be eliminated because CN VII (the facial nerve) is not directly related to the swallow mechanism, and CN IX (the glossopharyngeal nerve) provides sensory innervation that is more relevant to the initiation of swallowing rather than its coordination. Finally, CN V (trigeminal nerve) has no direct involvement in swallowing.

Predicted Answer: A: This scenario is most likely monochorionic-diamniotic twins because they share the same placenta but have separate amnios and sacs; the smaller twin has a higher risk for serious anomalies, including heart defects and growth restriction, which are hallmarks of this type of twinning. Furthermore, the newborns' weights do not indicate a significant weight disparity (more than 20%), which is typical for monochorionic twins with different amounts of shared placental tissue. The smaller twin has left-sided clubfoot, and the larger twin has polycythemia (increased hematocrit). This is consistent with a scenario in which the smaller twin has heart defects that are causing decreased perfusion to his/her lower extremities, leading to clubfoot. The increased hematocrit of the larger twin occurs because of intrauterine growth restriction, where the shared placenta had less surface area for the same number of fetuses being nourished, but also due to anemia in the smaller twin from decreased perfusion to the GI tract and skin; this decrease causes an increase in red blood cell production by the larger twin.

Predicted Answer: A: The pulmonary wedge pressure is equivalent to the left atrial pressure in patients without left heart obstruction and represents the central venous pressure plus any external pressures. It should approximate the right atrial pressure, as well. When both pressures are normal, then the right atrial pressure must also be normal (in this case, 0 mmHg). The RV pressure is equal to the RA pressure because they represent a single unit. A right ventricular end-diastolic pressure that approximates the pulmonary capillary wedge pressure would most likely occur when both chambers are functioning normally. Therefore, choice C is correct. Choices A and B might be seen in conditions such as cor pulmonale or severe cardiac failure of the left ventricle, where RA pressures are elevated, and right-sided heart failure, respectively. Choice D might occur if there was a significant pulmonary artery stenosis or severe tricuspid regurgitation. (This latter condition would lead to an elevated RV end-diastolic pressure because the patient's heart would be unable to contract against a severely increased RA pressure.)

Predicted Answer: A: The ATP2A1 gene encodes a specific protein that pumps Ca2+ ions into the sarcoplasmic reticulum during muscle relaxation. If this pump is defective, then Ca2+ ions will remain in the cytosol, resulting in increased cytosolic Ca2+ concentration. Because muscles are unable to relax and return to their resting state quickly enough, there would be an overall increase in muscle relaxation time. Thus, choice A is correct. Choice B is incorrect because it suggests that both muscle relaxation time and cytosolic calcium ion levels increase. This would occur if the sarcoplasmic reticulum were less efficient at pumping Ca2+ ions into the sarcoplasmic reticulum during active contraction rather than being defective in its ability to pump Ca2+ into the sarcoplasmic reticulum during muscle relaxation. Choice C is incorrect because the cytosolic calcium ion concentration would increase if there was a deficiency in this enzyme. Choice D is incorrect because it suggests that both muscle relaxation time and cytosolic calcium ion levels decrease, which are not expected outcomes for mutations in the ATP2A1 gene.

Predicted Answer: A: This patient's symptoms suggest that he is experiencing primary aldosteronism and needs an aldosterone receptor antagonist. However, spironolactone can cause gynecomastia due to its anti-androgen activity, making it potentially less desirable for men. Eplerenone, choice B, is a selective aldosterone receptor antagonist that will suppress mineralocorticoid but not glucocorticoid effects (unlike the other choices). It has less of an effect on androgen levels than spironolactone, although its side effect profile is otherwise similar to that of spironolactone. This makes eplerenone a good choice for men with primary aldosteronism and concerns about sexual dysfunction as side effects.

Retrieved Content: for 5 seconds inactivates the virus while saving itsimmunologic and nutritional properties.
Predicted Answer: A: Chlorhexidine is an antimicrobial agent that can inactivate a broad spectrum of viruses and bacteria. It does not readily degrade and maintains its effectiveness on a nonporous surface for up to 6 hours after application. This contrasts with ethyl alcohol (D), which has a short shelf life, loses effectiveness quickly when it is applied to a surface, and degrades completely within about 30 minutes on a contaminated surface, making it unavailable for continued antimicrobial activity. Chlorhexidine is also not commonly used as an antiseptic in the treatment of skin or mucous membrane infections because its application can cause local irritation. Sodium hypochlorite (B) is a strong oxidizing agent that readily degrades into water and salt when exposed to air, making it not useful for the disinfection of surfaces where residual activity would be beneficial. Sulfuric acid is too corrosive to be used as an antiseptic or on some surface materials, and even if it were applied under ideal conditions (e.g., at a pH that was not caustic), its strong acidity would degrade organic material on the surface of objects being disinfected, making it unlikely to leave residual antimicrobial activity.
The best answer is A.

Retrieved Content: of bacteria with high antibiotic resistance. Predicted Answer: A: A ciprofloxacin is a fluoroquinolone antibiotic. Fluoroquinolones inhibit DNA gyrase and topoisomerase IV by competing with Mg+2 for binding sites in the active site of these enzymes, preventing the enzymes from separating and rejoining DNA strands during replication and transcription. Resistance to this type of drug usually involves point mutations in the target enzyme genes. Thus, (B) is correct. (A) is incorrect because the bacterial cell wall will not protect bacteria against fluoroquinolone antibiotics since they inhibit enzymes critical for replication and transcription that are located inside the cytoplasm rather than on the cell surface. Replacement of D-alanine also does not provide resistance to this antibiotic, so (A) is incorrect as well. Ciprofloxacin binds to a site on its target enzyme outside of the active site, which is where the substrate and Mg+2 bind. The drug cannot inhibit DNA gyrase until it has bound to the enzyme, making an enzymatic inactivation mechanism impossible. Thus, (C) is also incorrect.
The best answer is B.

Predicted Answer: A: The symptoms presented here point towards a diagnosis of hypothyroidism, which is an adverse effect of medications that inhibit the synthesis of thyroid hormone. The most likely medication that this woman is taking is thionamide anti-thyroid drugs, such as methimazole and propylthiouracil, which inhibit the enzyme T4 to 3,5,3'-triiodothyronine (T3) conversion in the thyroid gland (T4 → T3), thereby inhibiting thyroid hormone production. Choice B is incorrect because these drugs do not inhibit serotonin reuptake; if anything, they decrease thyroid hormones that influence serotonin regulation. Choice C is incorrect because stimulation of norepinephrine release would actually increase thyroid hormone production rather than decrease it. Choice D is incorrect because inhibition of lipase activity in the pancreas or small intestine does not result in hypothyroidism as an adverse effect.

Predicted Answer: A: The Aa gradient is a measure of the difference in oxygen tension between arterial blood and alveolar gas. An elevated Aa gradient indicates an area of the lung where hypoxia exists due to problems with ventilation, perfusion matching, or diffusion capacity. This patient's symptoms and examination findings are consistent with heart failure (CHF). The physical exam findings of jugular venous distension and crackles in both lungs suggest that fluid is accumulating in the alveoli. In CHF, a left-to-right shunt develops due to increased flow through diseased pulmonary capillaries that are unable to accommodate normal flow, resulting in a situation in which areas of lung units have low ventilation per unit of blood flow (V/Q ratio). This situation results in an elevated Aa gradient because areas with high perfusion and low ventilation will have low oxygen tension. Thus, (C) is the best answer.

Predicted Answer: A: The clinical presentation is suggestive of hereditary spherocytosis. The osmotic fragility gap is normal, making it unlikely that this diagnosis is correct. The infant has a Coombs test negative anemia with hemolysis and a normocytic anemia pattern. This suggests the enzyme deficiency is likely glucose-6-phosphate dehydrogenase (G6PD). G6PD is important in maintaining the balance of NADPH and NADP+ within cells. An allosteric inhibitor of G6PD is adenosine monophosphate (AMP). AMP is an allosteric activator of PK, which will lead to increased glucose breakdown when AMP levels are high. When AMP is low, as in rest periods, PK is less active and the cell conserves energy. The normal physiological activity of G6PD is maintaining this balance and keeping NADPH levels high even during periods of low glucose availability. The question stem asks for an allosteric inhibitor of this enzyme.

82.4.5 Infection
Predicted Answer: A
Explanation:
The patient's clinical presentation and laboratory studies suggest a diagnosis of hereditary spherocytosis. Red blood cell transfusions would most likely have prevented this patient's oropharyngeal symptoms because they reduce the amount of oxidative damage caused by free iron released from hemoglobin in lysed red cells, thus preventing oropharyngeal ulcers.

The other options are incorrect because they do not address the pathogenesis of this patient's condition. Hereditary spherocytosis is a genetic disorder that results from mutations affecting proteins in the red blood cell membrane. Although it can be caused by abnormalities in genes (ie, CD44, band 3, ankyrin, or protein 4.2), none are associated with gluten sensitivity, vitamin B12 deficiency, or folate deficiency.

This patient's father had gallstones, which is consistent with a possible diagnosis of hereditary spherocytosis as it can be caused by similar genetic mutations affecting band 3 and causing an imbalance in the pH within certain cells. This results in an excess formation of bilirubin, leading to a variety of symptoms including gallstone formation.

Predicted Answer: A: This is an example of a parallel study because each group of patients has been randomly assigned to one of the four groups and will receive either nitrofurantoin for 5 days, nitrofurantoin for 7 days, cefpodoxime for 5 days, or cefpodoxime for 7 days. This design is known as a "parallel" because each group of patients follows its own treatment regimen in parallel fashion. A factorial study would also involve more than one factor (e.g., type of medication and duration of therapy). The between-patient randomized study is incorrect because it refers to the randomization of multiple groups versus individual subjects within those groups. A cluster-randomized trial involves clustering individuals into groups based on some variable such as hospital or clinic and then randomly assigning the group to receive one of two interventions.

Predicted Answer: A) This patient has a history of systemic lupus erythematosus (SLE), which is associated with antiphospholipid syndrome (APS). APS is characterized by both arterial and venous thrombosis. Antiphospholipid antibodies can lead to clot formation in otherwise normal patients. These antibodies are directed against phospholipids that are necessary for coagulation cascade processes to occur properly. A Mixing study, or dilute Russell’s viper venom time (dRVVT), will be normal and not prolonged if the patient has antiphospholipid antibodies (the mixing study is a control) because adding normal plasma with no antibodies “cures” the abnormal test result due to the phospholipids from normal plasma being present in high concentrations. DVT is also associated with Factor V Leiden, which will increase prothrombin time. The PTT mixing study is used for diagnosing heparin therapy and is prolonged if heparin is present, not antiphospholipid syndrome.

Predicted Answer: A: The high serum ammonia concentration in a 3-day-old infant, along with the increased orotic acid to creatinine ratio on urinalysis and absence of urinary ketones, points towards a diagnosis of carbamoyl diphosphate synthetase (CPS) deficiency, an enzyme involved in the urea cycle. This disorder leads to the accumulation of ammonia in the blood due to the inability to convert ammonia into carbamoyl phosphate in the liver. The elevated orotic acid levels in urine is a characteristic finding in CPS deficiency because carbamoyl phosphate should have been used up in the synthesis of citrulline, thereby preventing orotic acid from being broken down further into uridine monophosphate and thereby accumulating in urine. Therefore, this baby most likely has an enzyme deficiency in A: Ornithine and carbamoyl phosphate to citrulline.

Predicted Answer: A: The organism described is likely Pseudomonas aeruginosa. This gram-negative rod produces pyoverdin as a siderophore, which allows it to take up iron in the face of abundant iron-chelating substances such as transferrin and lactoferrin. These substances bind iron tightly but can be overwhelmed by large quantities of siderophores produced by bacteria (see Fig. 4-18). Pseudomonas species are oxidase positive, non-lactose fermenting, gram-negative rods. The primary cause of disease in such patients is the ability of the organism to form biofilms that protect it from antibiotics and host defenses.

Predicted Answer: A
Explanation: A The key findings on physical examination in this case are photophobia, lacrimation, and itching along with swelling that involves both eyes bilaterally. Given these symptoms, the diagnosis is conjunctivitis, specifically viral conjunctivitis given the lack of fever or any other systemic signs of illness. In a patient with an allergy to cephalosporins, first-generation (e.g., cephalexin), second-generation (e.g., cefuroxime), and third-generation (e.g., ceftriaxone) antibiotics should be avoided. However, the fourth-generation cephalosporin cefepime is safe for use in patients with severe penicillin allergies and may cross-react with other cephalosporins. Cefepime can be used to treat conjunctivitis if viral causes have been ruled out (e.g., PCR on conjunctiva). Thus, the best choice here is A, as a third-generation cephalosporin would be the best treatment option for bacterial conjunctivitis in this patient who does not actually have an allergy. This eliminates B and C since bacitracin and tetracycline are not typically used to treat this condition (and also note that topical or oral antibiotics may be indicated for other types of eye infections). D is incorrect because azithromycin would be contraindicated in the case where there is a true allergy to cephalosporins.
The best answer is A.

Predicted Answer: A: This patient's symptoms of avoidance behavior after being treated for her urinary incontinence would be classified as a specific phobia. The patient's symptoms have lasted longer than 1 month; thus, (B) is incorrect. Similarly, the avoidance behaviors are not limited to one situation, so (C) is also incorrect. While patients with specific phobias may experience anxiety or avoidance in more than one type of situation, they can still be classified as having a specific phobia. Thus, (D) is incorrect.

Predicted Answer: A) This patient's lab results indicate pancytopenia due to a decrease in production of blood cells from the bone marrow. Aldesleukin is an interleukin-2 receptor antagonist used for melanoma that works by suppressing T-cell activation, but it can also have immunosuppressive effects which would be counterintuitive given the lab results. Interferon beta (B) is a medication used to treat multiple sclerosis and works by activating immune cells. Filgrastim (C), however, is a granulocyte colony-stimulating factor used in cancer chemotherapy that stimulates production of white blood cells, but this would be counterintuitive given the lab results as well. Romiplostim (D) is an immunomodulatory medication used for myelofibrosis and thrombocytopenia that works by stimulating megakaryocyte production to increase platelet counts. However, these patients had decreases in all blood cell types and would be more likely to be started on a medication such as prednisone (not listed) which is an immunosuppressive corticosteroid. Predicted Answer: A: This baby was born at 27 weeks' gestation and shows signs of respiratory distress syndrome (RDS), including low lung volumes, air bronchograms, and ground-glass opacities on chest x-ray. The condition is often associated with hyaline membranes lining the alveoli on biopsy and is most effectively treated with surfactant replacement therapy. Bilateral pleural effusions can be seen in conditions like pneumonia or congenital heart disease; however, they would not be expected to present concurrently with RDS in this case. A cord culture showing corkscrew-shaped organisms (Candida) might indicate a congenital infection but is unlikely in an otherwise healthy neonate who has no other signs of illness such as sepsis, skin lesions, or lethargy. The finding of meconium-stained fluid on deep suctioning would be more consistent with a scenario where the baby was growth-restricted and had chronic hypoxia leading to premature bowel maturation, and is unlikely in this case.

Retrieved Content: renal and urinary abnormalities
Predicted Answer: A
Explanation: The most common cause of end-stage renal disease in patients with sickle cell disease is an autosomal dominant trait called sickle cell nephropathy or sickle kidney disease. Sickle cell nephropathy typically presents with chronic low-grade proteinuria, microalbuminuria, and impaired glomerular filtration rate (GFR) as the initial findings. This disease process usually progresses slowly over many years before end-stage renal disease is reached. Membranous glomerulonephritis,  (A), is a known complication of sickle cell disease. The patient's age, gender, and ethnicity all increase his risk for developing membranous glomerulonephritis. Proximal renal tubular acidosis,  (B) , and impaired urine concentration ability,  (D) , are not associated with this patient's condition. Impaired diluting ability, however, is a known complication of sickle cell disease as a result of the sickling process causing damage to the collecting ducts in the kidneys and impairing the ability to concentrate or dilute urine appropriately. The risk factors for developing end-stage renal disease include age greater than 35 years; proteinuria; hypertension; and family history of kidney disease. This patient is at increased risk for developing membranous glomerulonephritis based on his age, gender, ethnicity, and the fact that he has sickle cell disease.
The best answer is A.

Predicted Answer: A: The inferior vena cava is located near the level of L3. It would be the most likely structure injured in this case. The internal iliac veins do ascend as high as L3, but they are not the most likely structures injured because their course is more laterally positioned at this level. The renal arteries arise from the aorta and would be less likely to be injured from a penetrating wound below L3. Similarly, the portal vein courses through the liver and its location makes it unlikely that it would have been injured by a gunshot wound at the level of L3. However, because oral bioavailability is only 75%, the effective dose that is needed would be 360 mg / 0.75 = 480 mg.

Retrieved Content: medications for mothers of premature or otherwise compro-
Predicted Answer: A: Recurrent pregnancy loss is defined as three or more consecutive pregnancy losses before 20 weeks of gestation. The patient in this question has experienced four miscarriages and meets criteria for recurrent fetal loss. For patients with a history of recurrent fetal loss, the general consensus among experts is that treatment should begin at the time of conception to prevent another loss, rather than starting once pregnancy is confirmed through ultrasound. Aspirin and heparin have been shown to be effective in preventing miscarriages in this population. This combination has been studied extensively; it works by decreasing inflammation and clotting in the placenta. Although warfarin was previously used for pregnant patients with a history of recurrent fetal loss, the use of warfarin is associated with an increased risk of birth defects and should be avoided. Furthermore, rivaroxaban is an anticoagulant medication that has not been studied extensively for the prevention of miscarriages in this population.

Predicted Answer: A: This patient presents with signs of pneumonia, as evidenced by the cough, fatigue, crackles on pulmonary exam, and fever. Given his age and underlying comorbidities, he is a high-risk candidate for complications from this infection (e.g., respiratory failure, sepsis). As such, IV antibiotics should be initiated in the hospital setting. Although azithromycin can be used to treat community-acquired pneumonia, it is typically used as monotherapy when outpatient treatment is considered appropriate (Choice B). Midazolam, choice B, does not cause such reactions at any dose of administration. Desflurane, choice D, may contain trace quantities of a contaminant known as TDI (tetrachloro-p-xylylene), which can be hepatotoxic, but this side effect is exceedingly rare compared with halothane.

Predicted Answer: A: In this patient, the ristocetin cofactor assay is decreased, which would suggest a platelet issue as opposed to an intrinsic coagulation pathway problem. The decrease in factor VIII activity and prolonged activated partial thromboplastin time (aPTT) might suggest an inherited deficiency of either factor VIII or IX. However, it does not match the ristocetin cofactor assay findings. Factor XI deficiency would present with decreased aPTT values but normal platelet function. Antithrombin III is associated with heparin and other anticoagulant pathways rather than bleeding disorders. The bleeding disorder presented in this patient would most likely be due to abnormalities of the glycoprotein (Gp) Ib/IX/V complex, which are responsible for binding vWF to platelets. This condition typically presents as mild bleeding due to platelet dysfunction but can manifest with severe bleeding when the GpIb is affected. A deficiency in the GpIb would lead to a similar clinical presentation seen in this patient, and would also result in a decreased ristocetin cofactor assay, making (A) correct.

Predicted Answer: A: This patient has a case of septic arthritis due to Neisseria gonorrhoeae (gonococcus) that was acquired during her camping trip. Predicted Answer: A: The patient described in this question has what is called "nephritic syndrome," a clinical condition where there is inflammation of the glomeruli and acute injury of renal tubules. Predicted Answer: A: A 52-year-old female patient presenting with a headache that is worse in the morning, associated with mild papilledema and imaging findings of a solitary lesion in the temporal region predominantly hemorrhagic, most likely represents metastatic disease to the brain. This patient's history of hypertension and obesity could suggest primary renal cell carcinoma; however, the age and gender of this patient make lung cancer more likely as the source of her metastasis. Lung cancer is one of the most common sources of brain metastases in patients who are not small-cell carcinoma. Small-cell carcinoma tends to be aggressive with a predilection for the basal ganglia and corpus callosum, as mentioned earlier. Therefore, choice A is correct. Lung cancer typically metastasizes to the brain by the direct hematogenous pathway and less commonly through the cerebrospinal fluid (CSF). Melanoma can also metastasize directly to the brain but would be associated with skin melanomas or other visceral cancers like the GI tract, as mentioned earlier. Multiple myeloma is not a primary cancer that causes metastatic disease in the brain; however, it does cause a form of amyloidosis called AL amyloidosis. This can affect nerves within the brain and spinal cord as well as peripheral nerves. Thyroid cancer also has been known to be associated with metastases to the brain but usually is associated with papillary carcinoma. The most common presentation of metastatic disease from thyroid cancer would be a single lesion in one hemisphere (choice D). However, this would be very unusual given this patient's age and gender.

Retrieved Content: (isolated or                                               (retinochoroiditis, optic nervein combination)                                       atrophy) external eye

Predicted Answer: A: The narrowing of retinal arteries and microaneurysms seen in this patient are indicative of diabetic retinopathy. This condition arises from tissue-specific differences in expression of sorbitol dehydrogenase, which is more highly expressed in the retina than in other tissues. In the eye, glucose diffuses into the lens and retina where it accumulates due to a lack of insulin. Sorbitol dehydrogenase catalyzes the conversion of glucose to sorbitol within these cells, leading to the accumulation of sorbitol, which pulls water into the cell by osmosis. This causes cellular swelling and eventually retinal damage. (B), galactocerebrosidase deficiency leads to Krabbe disease, a neurological disorder with symptoms such as muscle weakness, seizures, blindness in infants, and eventual death. Patients who have this condition exhibit demyelination due to the inability of myelin-forming cells called oligodendrocytes to break down sphingolipids that are present in their cell membranes. (C), arylsulfatase A deficiency leads to metachromatic leukodystrophy, a form of lysosomal storage disease. Symptoms include seizures, muscle weakness, and mental retardation beginning at any age between infancy and adulthood. (D) sphingomyelinase deficiency is associated with Niemann-Pick disease type A, a rare genetic disorder that causes an accumulation of sphingomyelin in the brain, lungs, spleen, liver, and bone marrow due to the inability of myeloid cells to break down sphingomyelin from cellular membranes.
Correct Answer: A

Retrieved Content: 120.7.1 Disorders Presenting with Metabolic
Acidosis
Predicted Answer: A: The description of recurrent episodes since birth that resolved spontaneously in a patient who has poorly controlled diabetes mellitus and was recently started on glipizide should raise suspicion for allopurinol-induced hyperuricemia leading to acute gouty arthritis, which can cause dark urine, fatigue, and joint pain. Gout can be precipitated by several factors, including the initiation of diuretic medication such as hydrochlorothiazide and thiazides, but not glipizide. However, another type of medication may also be implicated in the patient's symptoms. The glipizide is a sulfonylurea used to treat diabetes mellitus. Sulfa drugs are known to induce hyperuricemia. Other side effects include fever, rash, arthralgias, and rarely agranulocytosis. Therefore, in this patient with poorly controlled type II DM on glipizide, the most likely cause of his symptoms is a sulfa drug-induced hypersensitivity reaction (a.k.a. Stevens-Johnson syndrome), which is also known as allopurinol hypersensitivity syndrome when it occurs after taking allopurinol for gout prophylaxis.

Retrieved Content: There are several characteristic ECG appearances: right
Predicted Answer: A: Atrial fibrillation is a chaotic, disorganized atrial rhythm that typically presents as a rapid, irregularly irregular rhythm on ECG. This condition can be precipitated by factors such as severe hyperthyroidism, cardiac surgery, myocardial infarction, and ethanol intoxication (as seen in this patient). The patient's presentation of palpitations and dizziness is classic for atrial fibrillation. Option B, Brugada syndrome, would present with a different ECG pattern, called the "Brugada sign," which consists of ST segment elevation in leads V1 and sometimes V2. This sign does not appear to be present on this patient's ECG. The patient would also likely present with sudden cardiac death from ventricular fibrillation as his first manifestation of disease. Option C, ventricular tachycardia, is a fast rhythm originating from the ventricles that can cause palpitations and dizziness but does not have an irregularly irregular rhythm as seen on this ECG. Option D, sick sinus syndrome, is caused by dysfunction at or above the SA node level, leading to heart block, bradycardia, or tachy-brady syndrome. This condition would likely present with a slow heart rate rather than palpitations and dizziness.

The best answer is A.

Predicted Answer: A
Explanation
This question requires a detailed understanding of cellular processes involved in stroke. Ischemia causes an initial drop in oxygen delivery and thus energy production. The area at risk is characterized by decreased ATP levels (choice D) as the penumbra tissue remains ischemic but still viable for several hours after onset. The mitochondrial permeability transition pore (PTP) opens early during ischemia, allowing ions to enter the mitochondria. Once open, this pore cannot close, resulting in cell death and a more significant injury than initially apparent. Caspase activation is typical of apoptosis, not reversible cellular injury (choice B). Lysosomal rupture would be seen with necrotic cells rather than those undergoing penumbra or "injury" changes (choice C).

The best answer is A.

Predicted Answer: A: The formula for calculating a loading dose of heparin is as follows:
Loading dose = (Target peak plasma concentration × Volume of distribution) The volume of distribution for heparin in this patient would be 60 mL/kg × 50 kg, which equals 3 L. If we plug that into the equation with a target peak plasma concentration of 0.5 units/mL, we get: Loading dose = (0.5 units/mL × 3 L) = 1.5 units × 3 L/mL = 4.5 units/L × 1000 mL/L = 4500 units. This choice is closest to the answer. Therefore, the correct answer is A.

Predicted Answer: A
Explanation:
Telomerase is most likely associated with increased activity in the cells of this patient’s tumor, a liposarcoma. Telomeres are nucleoprotein structures that cap the ends of chromosomes and prevent them from fusing together. Telomerase catalyzes the addition of TTAGGG repeats to telomere ends, which can lead to cancer if present at high levels in somatic cells (i.e., cells that are not part of germ lines). Cancer has been found to arise when telomeres are elongated by telomerase activity. Telomerase is inactive in normal somatic human cells but activated in the majority of cancers, including liposarcomas.

Predicted Answer: A: This patient's symptoms are most consistent with a hot tub folliculitis, caused by the bacterium Pseudomonas aeruginosa. The inflammation in this condition originates from the hair follicles of the skin, which makes choice B correct. A common predisposing factor for this infection is exposure to contaminated water (e.g., swimming pools or whirlpools). Hot tub folliculitis typically presents as numerous small pustules that develop several days after exposure to the contaminated water.

The remaining options are incorrect because they do not relate to the origin of hot tub folliculitis.
(A) The epidermis is the outermost layer of skin, and hot tub folliculitis originates from hair follicles deeper in the dermal layer. The superficial portion of the dermal layer contains sweat glands.
(B) This choice is correct because hot tub folliculitis begins in the epithelium lining the hair follicle (follicular infundibulum). After a short incubation period, the infection progresses to involve the rest of the hair follicle and the adjacent skin. 
(C) The papillary dermis is the superficial portion of the dermal layer, which contains sweat glands and nerve endings but does not contain hair follicles.
(D) Eccrine sweat glands are located in the dermal layer but do not relate to hot tub folliculitis. Apocrine sweat glands are related to axillary body odor caused by bacteria on the skin.

Predicted Answer: A: The tuberculin skin test is a method for diagnosing latent tuberculosis infection. It involves placing a small amount of tuberculin fluid just beneath the surface of the skin and measuring the resulting inflammation in the form of induration (swelling) after 48 to 72 hours. A positive reaction indicates previous exposure to Mycobacterium tuberculosis, although it does not confirm current active disease. In immunocompetent individuals with latent TB infection, the tuberculin response is due to the production of cytokines such as interleukin-12 (IL-12) and interferon-gamma (IFN-\(\gamma\)), which activate macrophages that are able to kill M. tuberculosis. T cells also produce these cytokines by interacting with antigen-presenting cells via their B7 molecules and CD28 ligands. This activation of macrophages impairs the growth of M. tuberculosis. The patient in this question has HIV infection, which is a state of immunocompromised. In such individuals, there are high levels of IL-4 produced by T helper 2 (Th2) cells that interfere with macrophage function and thus produce a negative tuberculin skin test result. Th1 cytokines such as IFN-\(\gamma\) are depressed in HIV infection, making the use of the tuberculin skin test less reliable for screening for latent TB infection. It is also worth noting that some individuals who have previously been vaccinated with the BCG vaccine may also have a positive tuberculin skin test because they have been exposed to a similar organism (Mycobacterium bovis) that is used in the BCG vaccine.

The best answer is A.
Correct Answer: A

Predicted Answer: A: This question requires a clear understanding of how dexamethasone testing can help confirm suspicion of pituitary adenoma (Cushing's disease), which can be confirmed by measuring ACTH levels in response to low- and high-dose dexamethasone suppression testing. A normal response is for cortisol levels to decrease with both low- and high-dose dexamethasone. Individuals with Cushing's syndrome have an abnormal response, characterized by increased cortisol levels after taking a low dose of dexamethasone (which would suppress cortisol production in normals) but decreased cortisol levels after taking the higher dose. A key feature of this question is that it tests the ability to interpret the results. The correct answer will result from understanding how Cushing's syndrome affects the body's response to dexamethasone suppression testing. Therefore, (A) is correct. Both low- and high-dose dexamethasone suppress cortisol levels in normal individuals; thus, (B), (C), and (D) are incorrect.

Retrieved Content: complications) rather than assuming that the cause is utero-
Predicted Answer: Explanation for correct answer: Karyotype of the fetus can be diagnosed by chorionic villus sampling, amniocentesis, or a postmortem examination after delivery in such cases. In the present question the karyotype is not mentioned anywhere. But the patient has been having a mass protruding from vagina and the USG has shown no fetal parts which suggests that it is an abnormal gestation (mole). So the fetus will have a karyotype of 46XX, i.e., two X chromosomes. In molar pregnancies, there are two types: complete hydatidiform mole and partial hydatidiform mole. The complete mole occurs when an empty egg is fertilized by a single X chromosome from the sperm (i.e., no maternal genetic material). This results in all cells being 46XY. However, it can also be completely formed from paternal chromosomes only with no maternal contribution or be triploid, i.e., having 69 chromosomes instead of the normal 46, with three sets of chromosomes, one set each from both parents and a third set which is entirely paternal. The partial mole contains some fetal parts in addition to hydropic villi. So, if we rule out option A (because we have no information about karyotype) and option C (because it's unlikely to be an XY fetus and besides the karyotyping for this kind of thing should have been given), then the remaining options B and D are to be differentiated as follows: The complete hydatidiform mole is entirely due to the fertilization of a single ovum by a sperm. Hence, all the cells in the molar mass will be 46XY or 69XXY (the triploid form). This rules out option D which says 46 XX.
Correct Answer: D

Predicted Answer: A: The changes that occur in respiratory function with aging include a decrease in forced expiratory volume, and an increase in residual volume and closing volume. Predicted Answer: A: A nonhemolytic febrile transfusion reaction is a minor and common complication that usually occurs shortly after initiation of blood transfusions, as evidenced by the patient’s symptoms in this case. Mild reactions are most commonly due to an allergic response to the IV tubing, needle, or anticoagulant rather than the actual blood product itself. Predicted Answer: A): The patient's symptoms are suggestive of internuclear ophthalmoplegia, which is a disorder caused by demyelination in the medial longitudinal fasciculus, a bundle of nerve fibers that coordinates conjugate horizontal movements of the eyes (conjugate horizontal eye movement). This condition leads to an inability to adduct one eye on lateral gaze. The patient has this deficit in her right eye when looking left and in her left eye when looking right. She also has nystagmus in the other eye when she looks in the direction that causes difficulty with adduction, which is consistent with internuclear ophthalmoplegia. The most common cause of internuclear ophthalmoplegia is multiple sclerosis. This patient likely had a mild concussion from her bike accident and an MRI would have shown evidence of this condition. B is incorrect because there are no focal findings suggestive of a tumor in the pons (e.g., weakness, ataxia). C is incorrect because Wernicke-Korsakoff syndrome, caused by thiamine deficiency, presents with confusion, gait disturbances, and memory loss; these symptoms are not present. D is incorrect because infarction of the brainstem would result in more severe findings (e.g., coma) than observed here. (Note: The patient's fundoscopy results indicate retinal vein occlusion or papilledema, which could be secondary to demyelination.)

Predicted Answer: A: The organism causing meningitis is Neisseria meningitidis. Protein A is a virulence factor for Staphylococcus aureus and is not likely involved in this patient's infection. Lipooligosaccharides (LOS) are components of the outer membrane of gram-negative bacteria, including N meningitidis, that can contribute to the disease by inducing fever, causing cell injury, and acting as endotoxins. Adhesive pili facilitate the colonization of the respiratory tract for Neisseria gonorrhoeae but not N meningitidis, which is the causative agent in this patient's case. Biofilm formation involves a large number of organisms working together to create a matrix that protects bacteria from antibiotics and the host immune system. This process is important for several bacteria such as Escherichia coli and Pseudomonas aeruginosa but not for N meningitidis.

Predicted Answer: A) The patient has taken acetaminophen but states she took "a few pills." This situation is a classic example of an Acetaminophen overdose where the quantity of pills was unknown and therefore potentially high enough to cause liver failure, which can occur 16-20 hours after ingestion. This makes N-acetylcysteine (NAC) appropriate therapy at this point. Activated charcoal should be administered within an hour of ingestion for maximal effect but is not going to help in this scenario due to the timing. IV fluids are indicated because the patient may have taken other medications and their hydration status is unknown, but they will not protect against acetaminophen toxicity. Syrup of ipecac was used to induce vomiting but it has been shown to be ineffective for most overdoses, can cause gagging, and even lead to aspiration in patients who are unconscious.
